## Chapter: Chapter 12: Rock Deformation

### Introduction

The study of rock deformation is a critical aspect of geology and geophysics. It is the process by which rocks are altered in shape, size, and orientation due to the application of stress. This chapter, "Rock Deformation," will delve into the comprehensive study of this fascinating field, exploring the various factors that influence rock deformation, the different types of deformation, and the implications of these processes for the Earth's structure and evolution.

Rock deformation is a fundamental process that shapes the Earth's crust and mantle. It is responsible for the formation of mountains, faults, and other geological features. Understanding rock deformation is crucial for a wide range of applications, from mineral exploration to earthquake prediction and mitigation.

In this chapter, we will explore the mechanical properties of rocks that govern their deformation behavior. We will discuss the role of stress and strain in rock deformation, and how these properties can be measured and modeled. We will also examine the effects of temperature and pressure on rock deformation, and how these factors can lead to different types of deformation, such as ductile and brittle deformation.

We will also delve into the implications of rock deformation for the Earth's structure and evolution. We will discuss how rock deformation can lead to the formation of faults and other geological features, and how these features can provide insights into the Earth's history and dynamics.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of rock deformation, combining theoretical discussions with practical examples and case studies. It is designed to be accessible to both students and professionals in the field of geology and geophysics, and to provide a solid foundation for further study and research in this exciting and important field.




#### 11.4a Introduction to Rock Failure Mechanisms

Rock failure is a complex phenomenon that can occur due to various mechanisms. These mechanisms can be broadly categorized into two types: hydraulic and geotechnical. 

##### Hydraulic Failure Mechanisms

Hydraulic failure mechanisms are primarily driven by the action of water. These mechanisms are particularly relevant in the context of river bank failure. The hydraulic processes at or below the surface of the water can entrain sediment and directly cause erosion. Non-cohesive banks are particularly vulnerable to this type of failure, due to bank undercutting, bed degradation, and basal clean-out. 

Hydraulic toe erosion occurs when flow is in the direction of a bank at the bend of the river and the highest velocity is at the outer edge and in the center depth of the water. Centrifugal forces raise the water elevation so that it is highest on the outside bend, and as gravity pulls the water downward, a rolling, helical spiral happens, with downward velocities against the bank (erosive force). This is most severe in tight bends, and the worst erosion will be immediately downstream from the point of maximum curvature. In cases with non-cohesive layers, currents remove the material and create a cantilever overhang of cohesive material. Shear exceeds the critical shear at the toe of the bank, and particles are eroded. This then causes an overhang eventually resulting in bank retreat and failure.

##### Geotechnical Failure Mechanisms

Geotechnical failure usually occurs due to stresses on the bank exceeding the forces the bank can accommodate. One example is oversaturation of the bank following a lowering of the water level from the floodplain to normal bank levels. Pore water pressure in the saturated bank reduces the frictional shear strength of the soil and increases sliding forces. This type of failure is most common in fine-grained soils because they cannot drain as rapidly as coarse-grained soils. This can be accentuated if the banks had already been destabilized due to erosion of cohesionless sands, which undermines the bank material and leads to bank collapse. If the bank has been exposed to freeze-thaw, tension cracks may lay lead to bank failure. Subsurface moisture weakens internal shear. Capillary action can also decrease the angle of repose of the bank to less than 45¬∞, which is necessary for stability.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these failure mechanisms, exploring their causes, characteristics, and implications for rock engineering.

#### 11.4b Hydraulic Failure Mechanisms

Hydraulic failure mechanisms are primarily driven by the action of water. These mechanisms are particularly relevant in the context of river bank failure. The hydraulic processes at or below the surface of the water can entrain sediment and directly cause erosion. Non-cohesive banks are particularly vulnerable to this type of failure, due to bank undercutting, bed degradation, and basal clean-out.

##### Hydraulic Toe Erosion

Hydraulic toe erosion occurs when flow is in the direction of a bank at the bend of the river and the highest velocity is at the outer edge and in the center depth of the water. Centrifugal forces raise the water elevation so that it is highest on the outside bend, and as gravity pulls the water downward, a rolling, helical spiral happens, with downward velocities against the bank (erosive force). This is most severe in tight bends, and the worst erosion will be immediately downstream from the point of maximum curvature. In cases with non-cohesive layers, currents remove the material and create a cantilever overhang of cohesive material. Shear exceeds the critical shear at the toe of the bank, and particles are eroded. This then causes an overhang eventually resulting in bank retreat and failure.

##### Hydraulic Undercutting

Hydraulic undercutting is another important hydraulic failure mechanism. It occurs when the flow of water under the bank is greater than the flow over the bank. This can happen due to various factors, including the depth of the water, the velocity of the water, and the shape of the river bed. When undercutting occurs, the water can erode the bank from below, leading to bank failure.

##### Hydraulic Bed Degradation

Hydraulic bed degradation is a process where the river bed is eroded due to the action of water. This can occur due to various factors, including the velocity of the water, the depth of the water, and the shape of the river bed. When bed degradation occurs, the river bed can be lowered, leading to a decrease in the water level. This can cause oversaturation of the bank, leading to geotechnical failure.

##### Hydraulic Basal Clean-Out

Hydraulic basal clean-out is a process where the base of the bank is eroded due to the action of water. This can occur due to various factors, including the velocity of the water, the depth of the water, and the shape of the river bed. When basal clean-out occurs, the base of the bank can be lowered, leading to a decrease in the stability of the bank.

In the next section, we will discuss the geotechnical failure mechanisms, which are primarily driven by the action of gravity and the properties of the soil.

#### 11.4c Geotechnical Failure Mechanisms

Geotechnical failure mechanisms are primarily driven by the properties of the soil and the forces acting on it. These mechanisms are particularly relevant in the context of river bank failure. The geotechnical processes at or below the surface of the water can cause erosion and failure of the river bank.

##### Geotechnical Toe Erosion

Geotechnical toe erosion occurs when the forces acting on the bank exceed the strength of the soil. This can happen due to various factors, including the weight of the water, the frictional forces between the water and the bank, and the cohesive strength of the soil. When these forces exceed the strength of the soil, the bank can fail, leading to erosion. This is most severe in tight bends, and the worst erosion will be immediately downstream from the point of maximum curvature. In cases with non-cohesive layers, the soil can be washed away, creating a cantilever overhang of cohesive material. Shear exceeds the critical shear at the toe of the bank, and particles are eroded. This then causes an overhang eventually resulting in bank retreat and failure.

##### Geotechnical Undercutting

Geotechnical undercutting is another important geotechnical failure mechanism. It occurs when the forces acting on the bank are greater than the forces acting on the soil below the bank. This can happen due to various factors, including the weight of the water, the frictional forces between the water and the bank, and the cohesive strength of the soil. When undercutting occurs, the soil can be eroded from below, leading to bank failure.

##### Geotechnical Bed Degradation

Geotechnical bed degradation is a process where the river bed is eroded due to the action of water and soil. This can occur due to various factors, including the velocity of the water, the depth of the water, and the shape of the river bed. When bed degradation occurs, the river bed can be lowered, leading to a decrease in the water level. This can cause oversaturation of the bank, leading to geotechnical failure.

##### Geotechnical Basal Clean-Out

Geotechnical basal clean-out is a process where the base of the bank is eroded due to the action of water and soil. This can occur due to various factors, including the velocity of the water, the depth of the water, and the shape of the river bed. When basal clean-out occurs, the base of the bank can be lowered, leading to a decrease in the stability of the bank.

##### Geotechnical Bank Retreat

Geotechnical bank retreat is the final stage of river bank failure. It occurs when the bank has been eroded to the point where it can no longer support the weight of the water. This can happen due to various factors, including the weight of the water, the frictional forces between the water and the bank, and the cohesive strength of the soil. When bank retreat occurs, the bank can fail completely, leading to a decrease in the width of the river.

#### 11.4d Implications of Rock Failure Mechanisms

The understanding of rock failure mechanisms is crucial in various fields, including geotechnical engineering, civil engineering, and environmental science. The implications of these mechanisms are far-reaching and can have significant impacts on the stability of structures, the environment, and human safety.

##### Implications for Structural Stability

The failure of rocks can have severe implications for the stability of structures built on or near them. For instance, the failure of a rock layer can lead to the collapse of a building or a bridge built on it. This is particularly true for structures built on unstable rocks, such as those found in river valleys. The hydraulic and geotechnical failure mechanisms discussed in the previous sections can lead to the erosion and failure of these rocks, posing a significant threat to the structural integrity of these structures.

##### Implications for Environmental Quality

The failure of rocks can also have implications for environmental quality. For example, the erosion of rocks can lead to the release of sediment and minerals into the environment. This can have adverse effects on the quality of water bodies, such as rivers and lakes, and can also impact the health of ecosystems. Furthermore, the failure of rocks can also lead to the exposure of hazardous materials, such as radioactive minerals, which can pose a threat to human health and the environment.

##### Implications for Human Safety

The failure of rocks can also have implications for human safety. For instance, the collapse of a rock layer can lead to landslides, which can pose a threat to human life and property. Similarly, the erosion of rocks can lead to the formation of unstable slopes, which can also pose a threat to human safety. Therefore, understanding the mechanisms of rock failure is crucial for predicting and mitigating these risks.

In conclusion, the study of rock failure mechanisms is not only important for understanding the mechanical properties of rocks but also has significant implications for various fields, including structural engineering, environmental science, and human safety. Therefore, it is crucial to continue research in this area to better understand these mechanisms and their implications.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of rock strength and failure. We have explored the various factors that influence rock strength, including the type of rock, its structure, and the environmental conditions it experiences. We have also examined the different modes of failure that rocks can undergo, such as fracture, shear, and fatigue. 

We have learned that the strength of a rock is not a fixed property, but rather a dynamic characteristic that can change under different conditions. We have also discovered that the failure of a rock is not a random event, but rather a predictable process that can be understood and managed through the application of scientific principles.

In conclusion, the study of rock strength and failure is a vital field of research that has wide-ranging implications for many areas of science and engineering. By understanding the mechanical properties of rocks, we can design better structures, predict natural disasters, and manage our natural resources more effectively.

## Chapter: Chapter 12: Rock Deformation Mechanisms

### Introduction

The study of rock deformation mechanisms is a critical aspect of understanding the mechanical properties of rocks. This chapter will delve into the various processes that rocks undergo when subjected to external forces, and how these processes influence the overall strength and failure of rocks.

Rocks, being the primary constituent of the Earth's crust, are subjected to a myriad of forces, both internal and external. These forces can be due to tectonic movements, weathering, erosion, and even human activities. When these forces exceed the strength of the rock, deformation occurs. This deformation can be in the form of fractures, cracks, or even complete failure of the rock.

The understanding of these deformation mechanisms is crucial in various fields, including geology, civil engineering, and environmental science. For instance, in geology, it helps in predicting the occurrence of earthquakes and landslides. In civil engineering, it aids in the design and construction of structures that can withstand the forces exerted by the rocks. In environmental science, it assists in understanding the impact of human activities on the Earth's crust.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles that govern rock deformation, including the concepts of stress, strain, and deformation mechanisms. We will also discuss the various types of deformation that rocks can undergo, such as elastic deformation, plastic deformation, and brittle deformation. Furthermore, we will examine the factors that influence the deformation of rocks, such as the type of rock, the magnitude and direction of the applied forces, and the environmental conditions.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a comprehensive understanding of the mechanisms of rock deformation and their implications for the mechanical properties of rocks. This knowledge will serve as a solid foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the mechanical properties of rocks and their applications.




#### 11.4b Role of Friction in Rock Failure

Friction plays a crucial role in rock failure, particularly in the context of fault mechanics. The concept of fault friction is a key component in understanding the behavior of massive earthquakes and the associated rock failure. 

##### Fault Friction and Earthquakes

The process of fault friction is a fractal operation, meaning it remains scale-invariant down to the smallest crystal. This implies that the behavior of massive earthquakes is dependent on the properties of single molecular irregularities or asperities. 

When two clean nano-asperities are brought together in a vacuum, a cold weld will result. This is due to the cohesive bond between the crystal tips. However, in nature, these tips are covered with a thin film of foreign material, primarily water. 

The removal of this water, by extreme drying, results in a significant change in the behavior of the rock minerals. They no longer exhibit "fault healing" or dynamic friction. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of earthquakes, as it is the loss of frictional strength that allows for the significant movement of rock layers during an earthquake.

##### Fault Healing and Friction

After a major earthquake, there starts a process known as "fault healing". This is a well-demonstrated phenomenon involving a slow increase in the static coefficient of friction. With our nano-model, it is a matter of slowly pushing away the junk for a good cohesive bond. With typical minerals and water, there is another mechanism, whereby the water causes stress corrosion and weakening of the main asperity body, allowing more plastic deformation, and more contact.

The most important aspect is that this bond-strengthening is time-dependent. For a fault being stressed to the point of an earthquake, these bonds begin to stretch and break. They do not have time to heal again. Once the critical distance has been achieved, there is a significant strength loss, and the fault begins to slide.

In conclusion, the role of friction in rock failure is a complex and multifaceted topic. It is a key component in understanding the behavior of earthquakes and the associated rock failure. The concept of fault friction and the process of fault healing provide valuable insights into the mechanics of rock failure.

#### 11.4c Case Studies of Rock Failure

In this section, we will delve into specific case studies that illustrate the principles of rock failure discussed in the previous sections. These case studies will provide a practical understanding of how the concepts of friction, fault healing, and fault mechanics play out in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The 1999 Chi-Chi Earthquake

The 1999 Chi-Chi earthquake, with a moment magnitude of 7.3, occurred in central Taiwan. The earthquake was found to have ruptured the crust along a northwest-trending fault zone, with a vertical displacement of up to 10 meters. The fault zone was found to have a high degree of frictional strength, which allowed for the accumulation of stress over time before the earthquake occurred.

The fault healing process was observed in the aftermath of the earthquake, with a gradual increase in the static coefficient of friction. This was attributed to the re-bonding of the rock layers at the fault zone, which was facilitated by the presence of water. The earthquake was found to have caused significant damage to the surrounding infrastructure, highlighting the importance of understanding rock failure mechanisms in earthquake-prone areas.

##### Case Study 2: The 2010 Canterbury Earthquakes

The 2010 Canterbury earthquakes, with moment magnitudes of 7.1 and 6.3, occurred in New Zealand. These earthquakes were found to have ruptured the crust along a northeast-trending fault zone, with a vertical displacement of up to 10 meters. The fault zone was found to have a high degree of frictional strength, similar to the Chi-Chi earthquake.

The fault healing process was also observed in the aftermath of these earthquakes, with a gradual increase in the static coefficient of friction. However, the earthquakes caused significant damage to the surrounding infrastructure, highlighting the importance of understanding the role of friction in rock failure.

##### Case Study 3: The 2015 Gorkha Earthquake

The 2015 Gorkha earthquake, with a moment magnitude of 7.8, occurred in Nepal. The earthquake was found to have ruptured the crust along a north-trending fault zone, with a vertical displacement of up to 15 meters. The fault zone was found to have a high degree of frictional strength, which allowed for the accumulation of stress over time before the earthquake occurred.

The fault healing process was observed in the aftermath of the earthquake, with a gradual increase in the static coefficient of friction. However, the earthquake caused catastrophic damage to the surrounding infrastructure, highlighting the importance of understanding the role of friction in rock failure.

These case studies provide a practical understanding of the principles of rock failure and the role of friction in earthquakes. They highlight the importance of understanding these concepts in earthquake-prone areas, where they can help in predicting and mitigating the impact of earthquakes.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of rock strength and failure. We have explored the various factors that contribute to rock strength, including the type of rock, its structure, and the forces acting upon it. We have also examined the different types of failure that can occur in rocks, such as fracture, shear, and fatigue failure. 

We have learned that rock strength is not a fixed property, but rather a dynamic characteristic that can change under different conditions. The strength of a rock can be influenced by a variety of factors, including temperature, pressure, and the presence of fluids. We have also seen how the failure of a rock can have significant implications for the stability of structures and the safety of people and the environment.

In conclusion, understanding rock strength and failure is crucial for a wide range of fields, including geology, civil engineering, and environmental science. By studying the mechanical properties of rocks, we can gain valuable insights into the behavior of the Earth's crust and the processes that shape our planet.

## Chapter: Chapter 12: Rock Deformation Mechanisms

### Introduction

The study of rocks is a vast and complex field, and one of the most intriguing aspects of this discipline is the understanding of rock deformation mechanisms. This chapter, "Rock Deformation Mechanisms," aims to delve into the intricate world of rock mechanics, exploring the fundamental principles that govern the deformation of rocks under various conditions.

Rock deformation is a critical aspect of geology, as it plays a significant role in shaping the Earth's surface and the structures beneath it. It is the process by which rocks are altered in shape, size, or position due to stress. This deformation can occur through various mechanisms, including fracturing, faulting, and plastic deformation. Each of these mechanisms has its unique characteristics and implications, which we will explore in detail in this chapter.

We will begin by discussing the basic concepts of stress and strain, which are fundamental to understanding rock deformation. We will then delve into the different types of stress that rocks can experience, such as tensile, compressive, and shear stress. We will also explore how these stresses can lead to different types of deformation, such as brittle and ductile deformation.

Next, we will examine the role of temperature and pressure in rock deformation. We will discuss how these factors can influence the strength and deformation behavior of rocks, and how they can lead to phenomena such as metamorphism.

Finally, we will explore some of the practical applications of rock deformation mechanisms, such as in the design of structures and the prediction of earthquakes. We will also discuss some of the current research and debates in this field, providing a glimpse into the cutting-edge of rock mechanics research.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of rock deformation mechanisms, combining theoretical principles with practical applications. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of geology, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of the fascinating world of rock mechanics.




#### 11.4c Role of Creep in Rock Failure

Creep is a time-dependent deformation mechanism that occurs in rocks under high temperatures and pressures. It is a critical factor in the failure of rocks, particularly in geothermal systems and high-grade metamorphic environments. 

##### Creep and Rock Failure

Creep is a primary mechanism of rock failure in these extreme environments. The high temperatures and pressures cause the rocks to deform plastically, leading to the gradual movement of minerals and the eventual failure of the rock. This process is particularly significant in the context of geothermal systems, where the high temperatures and pressures are sustained over long periods.

The role of creep in rock failure can be understood in the context of the concept of "effective stress". In a rock, the stress state is not uniform, and the effective stress is calculated as the difference between the applied stress and the pore pressure. In the case of creep, the pore pressure can increase due to the generation of fluids during the deformation process. This increase in pore pressure can significantly reduce the effective stress, leading to an increase in the rate of creep and ultimately, rock failure.

##### Creep and Rock Strength

The strength of a rock is a critical factor in its resistance to failure. Creep can significantly reduce the strength of a rock, particularly in the context of high temperatures and pressures. This is because the deformation process can cause the alignment of mineral grains, leading to a decrease in the effective strength of the rock.

The role of creep in rock strength can be understood in the context of the concept of "creep strength". The creep strength of a rock is the stress at which it begins to deform plastically. As the temperature and pressure increase, the creep strength of the rock decreases, leading to an increase in the rate of creep and ultimately, rock failure.

In conclusion, creep plays a crucial role in the failure of rocks, particularly in extreme environments such as geothermal systems and high-grade metamorphic environments. Understanding the role of creep in rock failure is crucial for predicting the behavior of rocks under high temperatures and pressures, and for designing structures that can withstand these conditions.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of rock strength and failure. We have explored the various factors that influence rock strength, including the type of rock, its structure, and the environmental conditions it experiences. We have also examined the different types of failure that can occur in rocks, such as fracture, shear, and fatigue failure.

We have learned that rock strength is not a fixed property, but rather a dynamic characteristic that can change under different conditions. For instance, the strength of a rock can increase with temperature, but it can also decrease with time under constant stress. We have also seen how the failure of a rock can have significant implications for geological processes and structures, such as faults and joints.

In conclusion, understanding rock strength and failure is crucial for a wide range of fields, from geology and geophysics to civil engineering and materials science. By studying the mechanical properties of rocks, we can gain valuable insights into the behavior of the Earth's crust and the processes that shape it.

## Chapter: Chapter 12: Rock Deformation Mechanisms

### Introduction

The study of rock deformation mechanisms is a critical aspect of geology and geophysics. It provides insights into the processes that shape the Earth's crust and the forces that drive these changes. This chapter, "Rock Deformation Mechanisms," aims to delve into the fundamental principles and theories that govern the deformation of rocks.

Rocks, being the primary constituents of the Earth's crust, are subjected to a variety of forces that can cause them to deform. These forces can be exogenic, such as those due to weathering and erosion, or endogenic, such as those due to tectonic processes. The deformation of rocks can result in a variety of geological phenomena, including faulting, folding, and jointing.

In this chapter, we will explore the different types of rock deformation mechanisms, their causes, and their effects. We will also discuss the mathematical models and equations that describe these mechanisms. For instance, we might consider the strain tensor, represented as `$\epsilon_{ij}$`, where `$i,j$` are the directional indices, and `$\sigma_{ij}$` is the stress tensor. The strain tensor is related to the displacement vector `$u_i$` by the equation `$\epsilon_{ij} = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\partial u_i}{\partial x_j} + \frac{\partial u_j}{\partial x_i} + \frac{\partial u_k}{\partial x_i} \frac{\partial u_k}{\partial x_j} \right)$`.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles and theories that govern rock deformation mechanisms. This knowledge will be invaluable for anyone studying geology, geophysics, or related fields.

# Mechanical Properties of Rocks: A Comprehensive Study

## Chapter 12: Rock Mechanics in Engineering




### Section: 12.1a Role of Rock Mechanics in Civil Engineering

Rock mechanics plays a crucial role in civil engineering, particularly in the design and construction of structures that interact with rocky environments. The mechanical properties of rocks, such as their strength, deformation, and fracture toughness, are essential in determining the stability and durability of these structures. Therefore, understanding these properties is crucial for engineers to design structures that can withstand the forces exerted by the surrounding rocks.

#### 12.1a.1 Rock Deformation

Rock deformation is a fundamental aspect of rock mechanics that civil engineers must understand. It refers to the ability of rocks to deform under stress, which can be quantified by their strain. The strain of a rock is a measure of how much it deforms in response to an applied stress. The strain of a rock can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\epsilon = \frac{\Delta L}{L_0}
$$

where $\epsilon$ is the strain, $\Delta L$ is the change in length, and $L_0$ is the original length of the rock.

Understanding rock deformation is crucial in civil engineering, as it allows engineers to predict how rocks will respond to the forces exerted by structures built on or in them. This knowledge is essential in the design of structures such as tunnels, dams, and foundations, which must be able to withstand the deformation of the rocks they interact with.

#### 12.1a.2 Rock Fracture

Rock fracture is another important aspect of rock mechanics in civil engineering. It refers to the breaking of rocks into smaller pieces due to stress. The fracture toughness of a rock is a measure of its resistance to fracture, and it is a crucial factor in determining the stability of structures built on or in rocks.

The fracture toughness of a rock can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
K_{IC} = \sqrt{\frac{E' \gamma}{\pi a}}
$$

where $K_{IC}$ is the fracture toughness, $E'$ is the effective elastic modulus, $\gamma$ is the surface energy density, and $a$ is the crack length.

Understanding rock fracture is crucial in civil engineering, as it allows engineers to predict the failure of rocks and design structures that can withstand this failure. This knowledge is essential in the design of structures such as tunnels, dams, and foundations, which must be able to withstand the fracture of the rocks they interact with.

#### 12.1a.3 Rock Mechanics in the Design of Structures

Rock mechanics plays a crucial role in the design of structures in civil engineering. The mechanical properties of rocks, such as their strength, deformation, and fracture toughness, are essential in determining the stability and durability of these structures. Therefore, understanding these properties is crucial for engineers to design structures that can withstand the forces exerted by the surrounding rocks.

For example, in the design of a tunnel, engineers must consider the deformation and fracture of the rocks that the tunnel will interact with. They must also consider the stresses exerted by the tunnel on the rocks, and how these stresses will affect the stability of the rocks. This requires a deep understanding of rock mechanics, which is provided by the study of rock mechanics in engineering.

In conclusion, rock mechanics is a crucial field of study in civil engineering. It provides engineers with the knowledge and tools they need to design structures that can withstand the forces exerted by the surrounding rocks. This includes understanding rock deformation, rock fracture, and the role of rock mechanics in the design of structures.
